Energy Solutions recommend making incentive payment conditional on the permit application, not the permit closure, in order to avoid conditioning payment on factors outside the installer’s control. Regarding promoting job access, CSE recommends the Commission adopt a stipend for contractors who employ trainee technicians from disadvantaged communities. GRID opposes this idea, arguing that opportunities should be available to all people from disadvantaged and low-income backgrounds, not just residents of disadvantaged communities. GRID further recommends the SGIP HPWH PA market the program to job training organizations and community-based organizations in disadvantaged communities to support local and targeted hiring. Regarding post-installation inspection, Energy Solutions, Sierra Club/NRDC, and BWC comment on the proposed decision that requiring use of SGIP’s traditional inspection protocols would be burdensome and would create a market barrier. * * *
R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mph90 programs with which the SGIP program may layer incentives (e.g. the TECH Initiative), and would require an unreasonably large portion of the SGIP administration budget. Regarding the promotion of job quality, CSTPC, JCEEP, Grid Alternatives, IBEW-NECA, and PAO support a wage floor. CSTPC, JCEEP, GRID, and IBEW-NECA recommend the Commission require participating contractors to provide a minimum level of benefits. 11.3. Discussion We adopt the use of an eligible contractor list to govern contractor participation in the SGIP HPWH program. The SGIP HPWH PA/PI shall ensure that an eligible contractor list is readily available on a dedicated website, and may, as appropriate, coordinate with other programs to link to this website and list. Finally, we clarify that all HPWH projects receiving SGIP incentives must provide proof of permit closure with the Authority Having Jurisdiction for HPWH installations. This may be provided after incentives are paid due to the possibility of delayed action by the Authority Having Jurisdiction.

## For more information on CalEnviroScreen, including the CalEnviroScreen map, please see the

R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mph93 An eligible contractor list and the accompanying requirements provides a reasonable compromise to the positions given in party comments. Our approach includes search criteria that will help promote job quality, ensure quality installations, and improve job access for those residing in disadvantaged communities. We do not deem existing California state CSLB licensure requirements to be sufficient for ensuring quality installations as these represent the lowest threshold to operate as a contractor in California. Due to the overriding need to ensure effective incentive layering, we modify SGIP’s traditional inspection requirements to align with those used in the TECH Initiative. This will prevent mismatched inspection requirements from creating a barrier that deters contractor participation while still ensuring SGIP HPWH units are correctly and safely installed. Due to the overriding need to maximize deployment of HPWHs we do not adopt parties’ recommendations to require SGIP HPWH contractors to offer a minimum wage or benefits package. At this early stage of developing the HPWH market in California, we do not want to deter contractors from offering this alternative technology to customers seeking a new hot water heater. We find parties comments in this area compelling but fear that if we require minimum wage or benefits packages at this stage, potential participating contractors will simply not encourage customers to install HPWH. Regarding calls from parties for the SGIP HPWH PA to track workforce data beyond what is already captured in SGIP, we recognize the scarcity of workforce data in the water heater sector. However, in D.20-03-027 we established a rigorous evaluation framework for the TECH Initiative and BUILD program, that will include, at a minimum, (1) cost per metric ton of avoided GHG emissions; (2) projected annual and lifetime utility bill savings; (3) number
* * *
R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mph94 of low-emission systems installed (BUILD program only); and (4) market share for eligible technologies (TECH Initiative only).60 In addition, D.20-03-027 lists many other optional metrics, which may, in the future, include workforce data.61 It is most appropriate, we feel, for the smaller SGIP HPWH program to build on any workforce data collection efforts developed for the larger TECH Initiative and BUILD program. We direct the SGIP HPWH PA and evaluator to assess what information is already being collected regarding SGIP contractors and to determine, in consultation with Commission Staff, what might be appropriate additional workforce and training information to collect. The HPWH evaluator and/or the SGIP HPWH PA are directed to then undertake a “low-touch” method (i.e., an online survey, requiring submittal of basic information the time of application or incentive payment, or similar) to collect additional appropriate information. The selected low-touch method may include a role for the SGIP HPWH PA in collecting workforce information, or this may be entirely undertaken by the HPWH evaluator. Additionally, the SGIP HPWH PA and evaluator shall cooperate with any larger workforce data tracking efforts undertaken using GHG emission allowance auction revenues, any TECH Initiative and/or BUILD program evaluation, and/or any similar umbrella evaluation effort. The SGIP HPWH evaluator shall also provide a minimal review of the effectiveness of the eligible contractor list approach and any recommended improvements.

R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mph95 12. Program Evaluation 12.1. Staff Proposal Because so many ratepayer and non-ratepayer funded HPWH incentive programs currently exist, the Staff Proposal recommends methods to estimate the GHG performance and/or the grid impacts of adopted SGIP HPWH incentives. To evaluate the SGIP HPWH program’s benefits, Staff proposed the following:  The SGIP evaluator should summarize all the benefits achieved by a SGIP funded HPWH. These benefits should include, but are not limited to, the total GHG reductions achieved by the SGIP funded load shifting HPWH, which includes reductions in therms or kilowatt hours, and the peak reduction benefits compared to a non-load shifting HPWH;  When layering of SGIP HPWH incentives and other incentives towards a single HPWH system occurs, the non-load shifting benefits (i.e., the efficiency benefits) of SGIP funded HPWHs should be attributed to those other programs; similarly, the load shifting benefits achieved by SGIP funded HPWHs should not be attributed to other incentive programs; and,  The program year 2021-2025 SGIP Measurement and Evaluation Plan should include a dedicated HPWH impact evaluation report. 12.2. Party Comments CSE comments that there is no clear budget available for evaluation of the SGIP HPWH program. CSE notes the important potential insights from the SGIP HPWH incentive program because of the mandatory load-shifting requirement. Other key evaluation areas, according to CSE, are impacts on customer utility bills and the effectiveness of the JA-13 standards. CSE recommends that the Commission allocate funding for HPWH evaluation purposes from within the overall SGIP program budget or, alternatively, that the Commission eliminate the commercial unitary HPWH system incentive budget and allocate these funds
* * *
R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mph96 for HPWH evaluation purposes. In comments on the proposed decision, CSE requests clarification regarding costs to update the existing SGIP database to track the $44.7 million in SGIP HPWH funding within the database. CSE asks whether these costs should be taken from the SGIP HPWH PA/PI administrative budget approved here or from the existing SGIP PAs’ administrative budgets as approved in D.20-01-021. Similarly, CSE requests clarification on the appropriate funding sources for a market assessment of HPWHs. Energy Solutions recommends the Commission require the HPWH PA to collect a variety of data during the incentive application process to support evaluation:  Site information (customer name, address, phone number and e-mail);  Equipment information (manufacturer, model and serial number);  Invoice number and project cost;  Permit number (if permit required by law given installation scenario)  Whether a panel upgrade was required;  Confirmation that the HPWH is programmed to follow TOU schedules;  Equipment performance information if model is not included on existing qualified product list;  Equipment performance calculator for central HPWH applications; and,  Pre-install and post-install photos that show pre-existing unit fuel type, newly installed HPWH and evidence that unit is setup to follow TOU schedules.

R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mph97 12.3. Discussion In D.21-11-002, we adopted four non-binding guiding principles on HPWH incentive layering and program evaluation, which are: (1) ease of participation, (2) complementary incentives, (3) non-duplicative attribution of program benefits, and (4), ongoing coordination amongst PAs and implementers.62 As discussed above, a primary challenge, and opportunity, for the SGIP HPWH program is that so many other ratepayer and non-ratepayer funded HPWH incentive programs exist. The challenge this multiplicity of programs creates is the need for strategic incentive layering and design of an evaluation approach that is fair and that avoids duplicative attribution of benefits. To address some of this complexity, the non-binding guiding principles regarding HPWHs adopted in D.21-11-002 distinguish between claiming or
* * *
> “attributing” benefits and “reporting” or summarizing benefits. Regarding
claiming or attributing benefits, the non-binding HPWH principles suggest that, in the case that just one ratepayer-funded program targets a particular benefit as its primary goal, that program should claim all credit for all achievement of that benefit, even if an installed HPWH system draws on multiple incentives. An example of this is that only the ratepayer funded energy efficiency program has as a primary goal kilowatt hour and therm energy savings targets. Regarding reporting on or summarizing benefits from HPWH systems using multiple incentives, our D.21-11-002 guiding principles suggest that program reports and evaluations addressing HPWH incentive benefits should
* * *
> “acknowledge the existence of other programs that may also be contributing to
## D.21-11-002 at Appendix A.
R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mph98 adoption levels and note that the metric being reported is also found in other program reports.”63 As we state in D. 21-11-002, “[t]his reporting will promote transparency around potentially duplicative goals and metrics without disrupting programs that are largely not duplicative.”64 We adopt the Staff Proposal’s first two recommendations for evaluation of SGIP HPWH benefits and the non-binding HPWH principles adopted in D.21-11-002. We direct the SGIP HPWH PA and Commission Staff to implement Staff’s recommendations and the non-binding principles when planning and performing SGIP HPWH evaluations. The Staff proposal states that “[t]he SGIP evaluator should summarize all the benefits achieved by a SGIP funded HPWH. These benefits should include, but are not limited to, the total GHG reductions achieved by the SGIP funded load shifting HPWH.”65 We clarify here that the baseline for estimating benefits from HPWHs using SGIP incentives and the total GHG reductions reported in SGIP impact evaluations from such installations shall include the GHG benefits from avoiding the installation of a natural gas water heater, i.e. the fuel switching benefits, as well as the load shifting benefits and, if applicable, any energy efficiency benefits. The evaluation shall present these estimated benefits as both total benefits and as benefits broken out by the estimated constituent contributions. Further, the evaluation report summarizing program benefits shall summarize at a high level the non-SGIP incentives available and used by customers using SGIP incentives, including the best estimates feasible at the time the evaluation is written of the incentive amounts offered and used. The SGIP
* * *

R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mph99 HPWH evaluator shall take other steps as appropriate to summarize and place in context the benefits of SGIP HPWH incentives, when considered on a standalone basis and when considered alongside other non-SGIP incentive offerings and tax rebates. * * *
R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mph100 broader SGIP evaluation advice letter process directed in D.19-09-027.66 However, we do not require an advice letter approval process for this additional SGIP HPWH evaluation work. As discussed in D.16-06-055, Commission staff direct SGIP evaluation work.67 The advice letter process we adopted in D.19-09-027 for approval of the SGIP evaluation plan did not change this direction. As such, and within the evaluation budgets adopted here and in D.20-01-021, Commission Staff are authorized to modify the SGIP 2021 – 2025 evaluation plan at any time to advance the goals of a complete and efficient evaluation process, including modifying the timing and scope of individual SGIP evaluation reports. If, given this direction, there are additional means to coordinate evaluation of the SGIP HPWH program with evaluation of the benefits of the TECH Initiative, the BUILD program and/or other relevant HPWH programs, Commission Staff should explore and, if appropriate, undertake such coordination if this serves to avoid duplication of evaluation efforts, and/or leverage personnel, database, and other resources. Regarding CSE’s question about the budget that should fund modifications to the SGIP database to accommodate the HPWH program, we clarify that the HPWH administrative budget we adopt here shall fund any such changes needed. The SGIP database will be key to ensuring a smooth interface between the SGIP HPWH program and the rest of SGIP. Using funds from the HPWH admin budget to update the SGIP database will give the HPWH PA/PI the flexibility to best implement needed database changes.

R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mph102 13. SoCalGas Motion to Strike Sierra Club/NRDC Attachments to Comments on OIR On July 27, 2020, SoCalGas filed a Motion to Strike, requesting that certain portions of Sierra Club/NRDC’s reply comments on the OIR be removed from the record. SoCalGas alleges that Sierra Club/NRDC’s reply comments on the OIR are procedurally deficient because they fail to address a legitimate issue raised in SoCalGas’s opening comments on the OIR, namely the appropriate baseline to estimate GHG emission reductions from HPWHs receiving SGIP incentives. SoCalGas argues that Sierra Club/NRDC’s reply comments are (1) speculative, irrelevant, inflammatory, and outside the scope of this proceeding; (2) improperly attack SoCalGas’s conduct as a prudent PA; and (3) seek to effectively prohibit SoCalGas’s speech, which is protected under the First Amendment.68 SoCalGas states that the information provided by Sierra Club/NRDC has no bearing on whether SoCalGas could act as a prudent PA for the SGIP HPWH program. In its response, Sierra Club/NRDC state that the comments that SoCalGas seeks to strike are relevant to the question of whether SoCalGas should have a role in administering the SGIP HPWH incentives because they disclose a
* * *
> “conflict of interest between SoCalGas’ business interests and widespread
electrification.”69 Further, Sierra Club/NRDC state that their comments regarding SoCalGas are not “speculative,” but instead are based on a published Commission decision70 and published Los Angeles Times editorials and articles
* * *
## SoCalGas’s “Motion to Strike Certain Sections of Sierra Club /NRDC’s Reply Comments on
the SGIP OIR,” July 27, 2020 at 2.
* * *
## _69 Sierra Club/NRDC, “Response to SoCalGas’ Motion to Strike,” August 11, 2020 at 1._
## _70 D.18-05-041, Decision Addressing Energy Efficiency Business Plans, June 5, 2018, at 139,_
141–144.
* * *
R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mph103 that describe SoCalGas’s activities organizing Californian for Balanced Energy Solutions (C4BES) and filing lawsuits against new clean energy rules that would reduce use of natural gas in California.71 Sierra Club/NRDC observe that SoCalGas’s assertion that it has been a prudent PA for SGIP battery storage systems does not predict how SoCalGas might act as a PA for HPWH, which generally will reduce natural gas consumption (if replacing a natural gas water heater), as opposed to SGIP funded electric battery systems and renewable energy projects, which will not. In its reply to Sierra Club/NRDC’s response, SoCalGas again asserts that
* * *
> “the use of irrelevant information seeks to chill SoCalGas’s protected speech.”72
13.1. SoCalGas’s Motion to Strike is Denied We deny SoCalGas’s Motion to Strike. We do not see a compelling reason to grant a party’s motion to strike testimony or comments of another party that the first party deems irrelevant. Indeed, many comments we receive may be less than relevant, but that does not warrant their being scrubbed from the record. Instead, this Commission accords great to no weight to parties’ comments and/or testimony as we deem applicable in varying contexts. Moreover, in this instance, we find the information provided in Sierra Club/NRDC’s comments potentially relevant to the question of the appropriate PA to administer SGIP HPWH incentives because it identifies actions taken by SoCalGas that seek to avoid reductions in natural gas usage in California. Unlike electric batteries using SGIP incentives, HPWH systems that replace natural gas
* * *
## Sierra Club/NRDC, “Response to SoCalGas’ Motion to Strike,” August 11, 2020 at
## _72 SoCalGas’s Reply to Sierra Club/NRDC’s Response to Its Motion to Strike, August 21, 2020_
at 3.
* * *
R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mph104 water heaters will reduce natural gas usage, which SoCalGas may see as a business threat. Thus, we regard Sierra Club/NRDC’s comments as potentially relevant and not improper, as SoCalGas alleges. However, we have accorded the information little to no weight because, as discussed in section 9.3, we adopt a single statewide SGIP HPWH PA for other reasons. These other reasons relate primarily to the need to streamline the program and coordinate incentive administration across multiple HPWH programs, including the TECH Initiative and BUILD program. SoCalGas makes a third point, that Sierra Club/NRDC’s “use of irrelevant information seeks to chill SoCalGas’s protected speech.”73 However, there is nothing chilling or restrictive about Sierra Club/NRDC’s inclusion of the contested information in their reply comments to the OIR. Further, as private entities, the Sierra Club and the NRDC do not have any obligation to preserve SoCalGas‘s free speech. SoCalGas filed both a Motion to Strike and reply comments to Sierra Club’s response to its Motion to Strike and has the opportunity to comment on the proposed decision addressing its motion. SoCalGas’s free speech rights have in no way been curtailed or impacted by Sierra Club/NRDC’s OIR reply comments. 14. Comments on Proposed Decision The proposed decision of Commissioner Clifford Rechtschaffen in this matter was mailed to the parties in accordance with Section 311 of the Public Utilities Code and comments were allowed under Rule 14.3 of the Commission’s Rules of Practice and Procedure. Comments were filed by CSE, BWC, PG&E;,
* * *
## Ibid.
R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mph105 SCE, Rheem, Sierra Club/NRDC, TURN, JCEEP, SoCalGas, A.O. Smith, Harvest, Energy Solutions, and SBUA on March 3, 2022. Reply comments were filed by SDG&E;, CSE, Harvest, CSPTC, JCEEP, A.O. Smith, SoCalGas and Sierra Club/NRDC on March 8, 2022. Demand Response: In response to comments by TURN, the final decision requires customers using SGIP HPWH incentives to enroll in a qualified demand response program, which we define as a CAISO market-integrated supply-side demand response program that counts for a load-serving entity’s resource adequacy obligations. This approach modifies our approach in the proposed decision, where we permitted but did not require HPWHs to enroll in demand response programs. Although Sierra Club/NRDC and SDG&E; reply comments suggest that IOU demand response programs targeting HPWHs with installed CTA-2045 ports and communication modules do not exist currently, we find, given the generous incentives we adopt here, that it is reasonable to require customers receiving SGIP HPWH incentives to enroll in a qualified demand response program even if the program does not employ communication with the installed HPWH. All customers are eligible to apply to participate in a qualified demand response program. Adding this requirement takes advantage of a one-time interaction with early adopter customers and will facilitate awareness about the benefits of and need for demand response programs, including offerings targeting CTA-2045 ports and modules installed in HPWH when these become available. Further, enrolling customers using SGIP HPWH incentives in a qualified demand response program provides immediate and additional grid support.
* * *
R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mph106 This requirement, triggered at the moment a customer makes a decision to install a HPWH using SGIP incentives, is an important opportunity to start making demand response participation more ubiquitous. It is not an onerous requirement. Customers enrolled in demand response programs typically receive additional incentives when they participate in a demand response event. Currently available qualified demand response programs include those involving customer enrollment via third-party demand response providers active in the Demand Response Auction Mechanism (DRAM) and aggregators active in the residential and non-residential IOU Capacity Bidding Program or in IOU-led programs such as SCE’s Smart Energy Program and Summer Discount Program, SDG&E;’s AC Saver Program, or PG&E;’s SmartAC demand response program. This list of qualified demand response programs is not exhaustive and will be updated as new offerings are established. Also eligible are market integrated demand response resources managed by third-parties and contracted by IOU and non-IOU load serving entities to meet resource adequacy obligations. To make the requirement meaningful, customers receiving SGIP HPWH incentives should stay enrolled in the demand response program for a minimum of three years. To facilitate expansion of demand response offerings for customers installing HPWH, the final decision adopts this additional guidance:  The Commission should consider the need for additional demand response pilots or programs involving or targeting grid-supporting HPWH, including HPWH with installed
* * *
R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mph107 CTA-2045 ports and communication modules, in the upcoming demand response application proceeding.74  The SGIP HPWH PA/PA shall describe in its implementation plan its proposed method to implement this requirement, which may, but is not required to, include the following approaches: (a) adding a statement to the SGIP application where the customer acknowledges the requirement (“by submitting this application I understand that I must enroll in a qualified demand response program for a minimum of three years as a condition of receiving SGIP HPWH incentives”); (b) including in the SGIP application an opportunity for customers to verify that they have already or simultaneously enrolled in a demand response program; (c) for residential unitary HPWH system customers, requesting that the relevant IOU verifies (and/or ensures) a customer’s enrollment in a demand response program concurrent with the IOU placing the customer on a TOU rate; (d) for residential central and commercial unitary HPWH systems, withholding issuance of incentive payments until the IOU verifies the customer is enrolled in demand response program.  The SGIP HPWH PA/PI shall, in collaboration with Commission Staff, offer an optional training and/or networking opportunity for contractors on demand response opportunities involving communication with HPWHs as these become available. Wide participation of contractors and third-party demand response aggregators and providers shall be solicited for this training or networking event. The purpose of this event shall be to educate contractors regarding demand response opportunities involving communication with HPWH and allow opportunities for the development of partnerships. The event may be promoted by and held in conjunction
* * *
## See D.17-12-003 at Ordering Paragraph 61; See also Executive Director letter dated September
30, 2021, extending the date for IOUs to file their 2023-2027 demand response portfolio
* * *
applications to May 1, 2022.
* * *
R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mph108 with the SGIP Technical Working Group and/or the TECH Initiative working group as appropriate.  The SGIP HPWH PA/PI shall include in customer application forms a question allowing customers to express interest in future demand response opportunities that target and/or communicate directly with HPWHs and that may provide additional bill savings.  The SGIP HPWH PA/PI shall provide contractors with information regarding qualified demand response programs and third-party aggregator offerings and shall update this information regularly. Information on available qualified demand response programs and third party aggregator offerings should be integrated into SGIP HPWH incentive application materials and program websites. In addition to these changes regarding our adopted demand response requirement, we have modified the final decision in response to party comments on the proposed decision as follows:  Eligible Technologies: In response to comments by Harvest and Rheem, the final decision clarifies that combination hot water heating/space heating systems and 120-volt HPWH systems are eligible for residential unitary incentives.  Additional Incentive for Larger Units: In response to comments by A.O. Smith, Energy Solutions, Sierra Club/NRDC, and CSE, the final decision adds an additional $700 incentive for residential unitary HPWH systems with storage capacities of 55 gallons or more. Party comments persuaded us that this additional incentive is necessary to help ensure right-sizing of customer units, which may need to have somewhat larger capacities than the water heater being replaced due to the pre-heating and load-shifting requirements we adopt in this decision. It is critical in this nascent stage of market development of HPWH in California that units are
* * *
R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mph109 correctly sized to ensure customer satisfaction and thus the long-term effectiveness of this program.  Eligible Costs for Equity Budget Panel Upgrade Incentives: In response to comments from A.O. Smith, Energy Solutions, Sierra Club/NRDC and CSE, the final decision modifies eligible costs for panel upgrade incentives for equity budget customers only. For residential equity budget customers only, we authorize use of electric panel upgrade incentives for additional eligible costs, not to exceed the incentive levels adopted here. The additional eligible costs we authorize for residential equity budget customers may encompass costs to change the location of the installed water heater within the residence or property to accommodate the larger size of HPWHs and/or plumbing or wiring upgrades needed in the residence for installation of the HPWH. Party comments persuaded us that excluding these additional cost categories from panel upgrade incentives for equity resiliency budget customers could present a barrier preventing these customers from participating.  SCE Execution of SGIP HPWH PA/PI Contract: In response to comments from SCE about our adopted statewide SGIP HPWH PA/PI structure, the final decision modifies and clarifies requirements pertaining to SCE’s role. Similarly, in response to comments from CSE, the final decision requires an upfront incentive funds transfer process from SCE to the SGIP HPWH PA/PI to ensure that monies are continuously available to the PA/PI to pay incentives to customers in a timely fashion.  Process for Customer Enrollment on TOU Rate: In response to comments from A.O. Smith, Energy Solutions, BWC, and CSE, the final decision includes changes to the process by which IOUs place participating customers on a TOU rate. The final decision requires the SGIP HPWH PA/PI to provide information to participating contractors regarding the rationale for the TOU enrollment requirement, namely that this will help ensure load
* * *
R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mph110 shifting for grid reliability and reduce customer electricity costs for water heating. The participating contractor must inform the customer applying for residential unitary SGIP HPWH incentives of the TOU requirement and must give the customer information provided by the SGIP HPWH PA/PI. It is then the SGIP HPWH PA/PI’s responsibility to notify the IOU about the HPWH installation and the IOU’s responsibility to reach out to and enroll the customer on a TOU rate within 30 days of being informed of the installation by the SGIP HPWH PA/PI. The parties presented compelling arguments that placing the requirement to report an installation to the IOU on the contractor could be a barrier to contractor participation.  Contractor Eligibility and Prioritization Criteria: In response to comments from Sierra Club/NRDC and Energy Solutions, the final decision reduces the number of contractor customer references required to be an eligible contractor in the SGIP HPWH program from three to two or allows the contractor to demonstrate that it has had two customer references verified previously in relation to a similar HPWH incentive program, such as the TECH Initiative. The final decision provides flexibility for the SGIP HPWH PA/PI to modify our recommended SGIP HPWH contractor search engine criteria as necessary to best achieve the goals set out in this decision. Energy Solutions comments persuaded us these changes would better promote program success.  State-Certified Apprenticeship Programs: In response to comments by JCEEP and CSPTC, the final decision modifies contractor search engine criteria to include prioritization of contractors for which 50 percent of installers have at least three years’ experience and/or have completed a relevant state-certified apprenticeship program. The final decision does not require all participating contractors to meet these criteria, however, as we believe this could reduce contractor participation, and expanding contractor awareness and participation at this stage is a central aim.
* * *
R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mph111  Contractor and Installer Training Requirements: In response to comments by A.O. Smith, Sierra Club/NRDC Energy Solutions, Rheem, JCEEP, CSPTC, and BWC, the final decision modifies contractor training requirements to state that completion of a relevant state-certified apprenticeship program, or completion of manufacturer, community college and other training courses meets our training requirements, in addition to completion of relevant IOU courses. The final decision requires the SGIP HPWH PA/PI to maintain a list of appropriate HPWH training courses and state-certified apprenticeship programs that provide satisfactory training. We were persuaded by these parties that the proposed decision had too narrowly defined eligible training.  Installation Inspection Requirements: In response to comments by BWC, Sierra Club/NRDC and Energy Solutions, the final decision modifies SGIP HPWH installation inspection requirements to align them with those approved for the TECH Initiative, which allows remote inspections. Party comments persuaded us that remote inspections are more appropriate for HPWH installations than the more detailed inspection requirements for SGIP behind-the-meter renewable generation and energy storage units and will be sufficient to ensure that SGIP HPWHs are correctly and safely installed.  Timing of Incentive Payments: In response to comments from Sierra Club/NRDC and Energy Solutions, the final decision modifies the timing of incentive payment to the contractor to place this prior to rather than after permit closure. These parties comments persuaded us that delaying incentive payment until after permit closure would deter contractor participation and is aligned with other ratepayer incentive programs.  Statewide SGIP HPWH PA/PI Bidder Criteria: In response to comments by Energy Solutions, Sierra Club/NRDC and CSE, the final decision adds two additional bidder criteria
* * *
R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mph112 for the statewide HPWH PA/PI RFP process as follows: (a) bidders should propose approaches for how successful layering of SGIP incentives with other programs shall be achieved in practice; and (b) bidders should describe how they intend to ensure HPWH adoption results in utility bill savings for participants in the residential equity customer class. These are valuable additional criteria that will support the SGIP HPWH PA/PI selection process and program success.  Thermostatic Mixing Valve Requirement: In response to comments from Energy Solutions, the final decision clarifies that thermostatic mixing valves are required as part of JA13 compliance and that apartment panels for single apartments are eligible for upgrade incentives.  Use of HPWH Evaluation and Administrative Budgets: In response to comments from CSE, the final decision in section 12.3 clarifies that the HPWH evaluation funds authorized here shall supplement the larger SGIP evaluation budgets adopted in D.20-01-021. Evaluation activities for SGIP HPWH shall be grouped with the other SGIP evaluation activities under the same contract. Commission Staff shall take all necessary steps to ensure this. We do not require an advice letter approval process for this additional SGIP HPWH evaluation work. We clarify that Commission staff direct SGIP evaluation work. As such, and within the evaluation budgets adopted here and in D.20-01-021, Commission Staff are authorized to modify the SGIP 2021 – 2025 evaluation plan at any time to advance the goals of a complete and efficient evaluation process, including modifying the timing and scope of individual SGIP evaluation reports. If, given this direction, there are additional means to coordinate evaluation of the SGIP HPWH program with evaluation of the benefits of the TECH Initiative, the BUILD program and/or other relevant HPWH programs, Commission Staff should explore and, if appropriate, undertake such coordination if this serves to avoid duplication of evaluation efforts, and/or leverage personnel, database, and other resources.
* * *
R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mph113 Regarding CSE’s question about the budget that should fund modifications to the SGIP database to accommodate the HPWH program, we clarify that the HPWH administrative budget we adopt here shall fund any such changes needed. The SGIP database will be key to ensuring a smooth interface between the SGIP HPWH program and the rest of SGIP. Using funds from the HPWH admin budget to update the SGIP database will give the HPWH PA/PI the flexibility to best implement needed database changes.  Non-Disclosure Agreements: In response to comments from SCE, the final decision limits required non-disclosure agreements between the selected SGIP HPWH PA/PI and other HPWH programs to the TECH Initiative and BUILD program.  Authorized Uses of Cap-and-Trade Allowance Proceeds: In response to comments from TURN and SoCalGas, the final decision provides greater detail regarding authorized uses of Cap-and-Trade allowance proceeds as included in CCR Section 95893(d)(3). We decline to make other modifications requested by parties for the following reasons:  GWP Kicker Incentive: The final decision declines to modify the low GWP kicker incentive as requested by several manufacturers but clarifies that the kicker applies to refrigerants with a GWP of 150 or less. 15. Assignment of Proceeding Clifford Rechtschaffen is the assigned Commissioner and Cathleen A. Fogel is the assigned Administrative Law Judge in this proceeding. Findings of Fact 1. Residential integrated HPWH systems are common in the U.S. but mostly use the refrigerant R-134a, which has a very high GWP of 1,430. Residential split HPWH systems are in limited use in the U.S. but common internationally; these
* * *
R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mph114 systems typically use the extremely low GWP refrigerant R-744 and operate at a high efficiency in a range of temperature conditions. 2. Multi-family buildings comprise most housing eligible for the equity budget in California. 3. Multi-family HPWH systems are typically composed of integrated central systems using very low GWP refrigerants. 4. Flexibility is gained by combining Staff’s proposed residential HPWH technology budgets into a general market and an equity budget, with both providing incentives for residential unitary and residential central HPWHs on a first-come, first-served basis. Combining the budgets this way will help the program reach as many customers as possible, will decrease the chance that the funds go unspent, and allows for greater flexibility for participation of multi family buildings. 5. Capping incentives for residential central HPWH systems at 40 percent of each of the residential general and residential equity budgets until such time as a preliminary review of these technologies’ load shifting performance is completed supports development of HPWH technologies in this customer class while ensuring compliance with statute. 6. Increasing the HPWH administrative budget to a cap of 10 percent of the $44.7 million in HPWH program funds adopted in D.19-09-027 and D.20-01-021 is necessary to address additional anticipated costs including initial set up costs for the program, HPWH evaluation costs, and any HPWH marketing, education and outreach costs, and is consistent with our approach to the TECH Initiative. 7. To ensure sufficient HPWH evaluation budget, it is reasonable to allocate approximately 25 percent of the HPWH administrative budget, or approximately $1.12 million, to evaluating the SGIP HPWH program.
* * *
R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mph115 8. Authorizing gas Cap-and-Trade allowance proceeds to fund additional SGIP HPWH incentives will primarily benefit residential gas ratepayers. The benefits will primarily accrue to gas customers receiving incentives because, if they want to install a HPWH, they may do so at a greatly reduced cost. 9. Gas customers utilizing SGIP HPWH incentives funded using gas Cap and- Trade allowance proceeds will benefit from avoidance of combustion of gas for water heating in the residence, with potential improvements in indoor air quality and health outcomes. 10. All gas ratepayers benefit from accelerating the development of the HPWH market in California through use of gas Cap-and-Trade allowance proceeds for SGIP HPWH incentives because SGIP incentives will help ensure HPWH technologies is more readily available, at lower cost, even after the incentives are exhausted. 11. Incentivizing HPWH installations as an alternative to gas hot water heaters will reduce GHGs. 12. In SB 1477 (2018), the legislature authorized use of natural gas Cap-and Trade funds for HPWH incentives in the TECH Initiative and BUILD program. 13. Allocating $40 million of 2023 gas Cap-and Trade allowance proceeds reduces the Climate Credit returned to gas customers in 2023 by a small amount, but it is expected that the average residential customer of each of the funding utilities will still receive a Climate Credit that will cover at least the full amount of costs that the gas utilities collected from them for Cap-and-Trade program compliance costs. 14. Delegating authority to Energy Division staff to reduce the SGIP HPWH allocation from 2023 Cap-and-Trade allowance proceeds if necessary to ensure
* * *
R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mph116 that the average residential customer’s Climate Credit is at least equal to residential customers’ annual Cap-and-Trade program costs is reasonable. 15. The fund allocation and transfer processes for 2023 Cap-and-Trade allowance proceeds are reasonable. 16. Authorizing the SGIP HPWH PA to propose via advice letter budget shifts between customer classes, new residential unitary and central system product qualification pathways, changes to the residential unitary system GWP kicker incentive, and use of AHRI communication device standards for residential unitary systems adds flexibility and helps ensure program success. 17. Authorizing an additional $700 incentive for residential unitary HPWH with storage capacities of 55 gallons or more will help ensure right-sizing of customer HPWH units, which may need to have somewhat larger capacities than the water heater being replaced due to the pre-heating and load-shifting requirements adopted here. It is critical at this nascent stage of HPWH market development that units are correctly sized to ensure customer satisfaction and thus the long-term effectiveness of this program. 18. Authorizing the SGIP HPWH PA to propose via a Tier 2 advice letter reductions to the incentive levels adopted in this decision at any time will maximize the effectiveness of incentive layering and incentive coordination work generally. 19. Requiring a range of appropriate connected communication devices as part of residential unitary HPWH appliance requirements and requiring customers to enroll in a TOU rate helps ensure that load shifting will occur. 20. Requiring customers using residential unitary HPWH incentives to enroll in any TOU rate, not just an SGIP-approved TOU rate, avoids creating an additional barrier for this less developed market and is reasonable given that the
* * *
R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mph117 modelling that informed adoption of SGIP-approved TOU rates in D.19-08-001 was based on the operation of battery storage units only, not HPWHs. 21. It is reasonable to require the SGIP HPWH PA/PI to provide information to participating contractors regarding our adopted TOU requirement and to report to the utility the installation of a residential unitary HPWH system using SGIP incentives; it is similarly reasonable for the IOU to then enroll the customer on a TOU rate within 30 days, and for the HPWH PA to disburse incentive payments to the contractor only after these steps have occurred. 22. Waiving the existing equity budget eligibility requirement that single-family homes be subject to resale restrictions or presumed resale restrictions for the purposes of the SGIP HPWH budgets removes a barrier that could unduly limit the participation of lower-income single-family households and is justifiable due to the somewhat larger dollar investment required for electrochemical energy storage as compared to HPWHs. 23. The GHG benefits from installing a HPWH system are estimated to be significant regardless of the type of appliance previously installed. 24. Limiting eligibility for SGIP HPWH panel upgrade incentives for residential unitary systems to households with existing panels of less than 200 amps and prohibiting incentives for panel upgrade costs that exceed the cost to upgrade to 200 amps ensures that SGIP incentive funds will not be used to support other electric equipment such as hot tubs or high electricity users more generally. 25. Adopting electric panel upgrade incentives that cover 100 percent of equity customer panel upgrade costs and 50 percent of general market customer costs helps ensure that equity budget customers can participate in HPWH
* * *
R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mph118 incentive offerings while still supporting general market customer participation and market growth. 26. An installed HPWH comprises a new and permanent load as defined under Electric Tarif Rule 15 (Distribution Line Extensions) and Rule 16 (Service Extensions). 27. Distribution or service line upgrade costs to ensure the necessary amperage to support a HPWH system may exceed Rule 15 and Rule 16 Electric Service Line Allowance caps for a very small percentage of customers. It is reasonable to require the IOUs to implement a Common Treatment for Excess HWPH Costs approach in these limited cases, for residential buildings, given California’s GHG emission reduction and decarbonization goals and the unequal impact that differentiated line upgrade costs can have on individual customers, who bear no responsibility for these costs. 28. Over twenty ratepayer and non-ratepayer HPWH incentive programs exist in California or will soon. 29. Providing some general guidance on incentive layering but then authorizing the SGIP HPWH PA the flexibility to determine, in collaboration with Commission Staff, the final incentive layer approaches across technologies and customer classes, including a contractor pass-through incentive if deemed necessary, will have the best results. 30. There is limited information available on the load shifting capacities and the consequent GHG emission reductions of central HPWH systems in multi-family buildings. 31. Adopting installation requirements for residential central system HPWH systems based primarily on the system’s projected load shift in kilowatt hours
* * *
R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mph119 and, secondarily, on projected GHG emission reductions reflects load shifting as the primary focus of SGIP HPWH incentives. 32. Requiring residential central HPWHs to be designed and installed to load shift energy from peak to off-peak periods in a manner that is projected to annually reduce GHG emissions by five kilograms of CO2 per kilowatt hour as compared to non-load-shifting electric water heaters helps ensure load shifting and GHG emission reductions will occur, but provides flexibility, which is necessary due to potential variability in multi-family building residents’ use of hot water. 33. Adopting performance-based incentives for residential central HPWH systems would create a market barrier and could discourage contractors from participating. 34. Linking residential central HPWH system incentive levels to the anticipated kilowatt hours of load shift services that the system will provide, with added quality control mechanisms, is reasonable because it is difficult for developers or engineers to fully control the variability of water use patterns, and resulting achieved GHG emission reductions, amongst residents of multi-family buildings. 35. Requiring all residential central HPWH systems using SGIP incentives to install equipment that enables data collection through two-way communication will help enable appropriate oversight of load shifting and GHG emission reduction claims. 36. It is inappropriate to provide electric panel or electric service upgrade incentives for residential central HPWH installations in multi-family buildings or commercial unitary HPWH systems as such costs could be considerable and
* * *
R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mph120 should be borne by the project beneficiaries, who generally have access to other financing. 37. Many commercial buildings have limited hot water demand and do not need a six-kilowatt capacity nominal compressor output level. 38. Authorizing the SGIP HPWH PA the flexibility to refine Staff’s proposed residential central and commercial unitary HPWH incentive approaches and load shifting requirements when developing standardized protocols helps ensure the program’s success and effectiveness. 39. Commercial unitary HPWH systems typically will not have a developer overseeing a continuous monitoring system and will not be receiving a GHG signal, so adopting a performance-based incentive approach is potentially problematic in terms of ascertaining the exact level of GHG emission reductions achieved and any related impact on an incentive payment. 40. Ensuring that small businesses have access to SGIP HPWH incentives allows this market segment access to program benefits and contributes to market development of relevant technologies. 41. Both the residential unitary and small business unitary HPWH GWP kicker incentives are appropriately sized to drive the market towards low GWP units, thus limiting the associated indirect GHG emissions. 42. Requiring the SGIP HPWH PA/PI to monitor regulatory developments regarding HPWH refrigerants and to track the type of refrigerant used in each SGIP HPWP project ensures inclusion of this information in SGIP evaluation report(s). 43. It is appropriate that commercial unitary HPWH system customers are ineligible for the electrical service line and associated electrical distribution
* * *
R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mph121 infrastructure cost classification as common facilities costs given these customers’ greater access to financing. 44. It is appropriate that commercial central HPWHs are ineligible for SGIP incentives given the uncertain ability of this configuration to shift load from peak to off-peak periods. 45. Centralization of SGIP HPWH incentive administration in a single statewide PA/PI aligns well with the existing TECH Initiative and BUILD program single PA approach, as well with the existing SOMAH program, and best provides for the streamlining and coordination of incentive offerings across the many HPWH incentives available. 46. The unique technologies and requirements of the SGIP HPWH program will benefit from a dedicated PA/PI that can devote its attention only to this aspect of the SGIP program. 47. The SGIP HPWH PA/PI bidder selection criteria proposed by Staff are reasonable, with some modifications. 48. An eligible contractor list will help promote job quality, ensure quality installations, and improve job access for those residing in disadvantaged communities if it includes tracking and prioritization of the criteria listed in section 11.3 in response to customer searches and if the SGIP HPWH PA/PI is authorized the flexibility to modify the recommended search criteria as deemed necessary to support program success. 49. Authoring the SGIP HPWH PA/PI to implement a post-installation inspection protocol that differs from SGIP’s traditional inspection protocol will ensure units are correctly and safely installed, will support effective incentive layering, and will ensure that differing inspection protocols across programs do not create a barrier to contractor participation.
* * *
R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mph122 50. Requiring SGIP HPWH contractors to offer a minimum wage or benefits package at this early stage of developing the HPWH market in California could deter contractors from offering this alternative technology to customers seeking a new hot water heater. 51. We do not deem existing California state CSLB licensure requirements to be sufficient for ensuring quality SGIP-funded HPWH installations as these requirements represent the lowest threshold to operate as a contractor in California. 52. There is limited workforce data available about HPWHs, but the SGIP program should collect only limited amounts of such data in conjunction with similar efforts anticipated for the TECH Initiative and BUILD program. 53. A challenge for the SGIP HPWH program is that so many other ratepayer and non-ratepayer funded HPWH incentive programs exist. This creates a need for strategic incentive layering and a benefits evaluation approach that is fair and that avoids duplicative attribution. 54. It is reasonable that SGIP impact evaluations report on the total GHG reduction benefits installed from HPWHs, including GHG benefits from fuel switching and from energy efficiency, if applicable, but that the credit or attribution of energy efficiency benefits from such units, if any, are claimed only by IOU energy efficiency programs. 55. We do not see a compelling reason to grant a party’s motion to strike testimony or comments of another party that the first party deems irrelevant. 56. SoCalGas’s free speech rights have in no way been curtailed or impacted by Sierra Club/NRDC’s OIR reply comments.
* * *
R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mph123 57. Given the generous incentives we adopt here, it is reasonable to require customers receiving SGIP HPWH incentives to enroll in a qualified demand response program. 58. All customers are eligible to apply to participate in a qualified demand response program. 59. Requiring customer receiving SGIP HPWH incentives to enroll in a qualified demand response program takes advantage of a one-time interaction with early adopter customers and will facilitate awareness about the benefits of and need for demand response programs, including offerings targeting CTA 2045 ports and modules installed in HPWH when these become available. 60. Enrolling customers using SGIP HPWH incentives in a qualified demand response program provides immediate and additional grid support. 61. The moment a customer makes a decision to install a HPWH is an important opportunity to start making demand response participation more ubiquitous. 62. Requiring customers receiving SGIP HPWH incentives to enroll in a qualified demand response program is not an onerous requirement because customers typically receive additional incentives for participation in the program. 63. Currently available qualified demand response programs include those involving customer enrollment via third-party demand response providers active in the DRAM and aggregators active in the residential Capacity Bidding Program or IOU-led programs such as SCE’s Smart Energy Program and Summer Discount Program, SDG&E;’s AC Saver Program, or PG&E;’s SmartAC demand response program. Also eligible are market integrated demand response resources managed by third-parties and contracted by IOU and
* * *
R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mph124 non-IOU load serving entities to meet resource adequacy obligations. This list is not exhaustive and will be updated as new offerings are established. 64. It is reasonable to adopt guidance that will facilitate expansion of demand response offerings for customers installing HPWH. Conclusions of Law 1. The Commission should adopt the SGIP HPWH budget allocations, incentives and program requirements contained in Appendix A. 2. The Commission should adopt Staff’s proposed single statewide SGIP HPWH PA/PI approach as described in section 9. 3. The Commission should direct SCE to enter into a contract with the selected vendor for the SGIP HPWH PA/PI as described in section 9. 4. The Commission should cap the SGIP HPWH administrative budget at 10 percent of $44,670,000, or $4,467,000 million, approximately 25 percent of which, about $1.12 million, should be allocated to evaluating the SGIP HPWH program. 5. The Commission should require, if the selected bid to administer and implement the SGIP HPWH program is below the 10 percent cap, after deducting the funds authorized for evaluating SGIP HPWH incentives, that the difference is reallocated equally across incentive budgets. 6. The Commission should cap the total percent of the residential general market and residential equity budgets that may fund residential central HPWH incentives at 40 percent of each of these budgets and should require the SGIP HPWH PA to take two steps if and when applications for residential central HPWH incentives meet or exceed 40 percent of either of these budgets: (1) initiate a waitlist for residential central HPWH applications in the relevant budget until such time as further guidance is provided by this Commission; and,
* * *
R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mph125 (2) prepare a short summary report of the load shift performance of approved residential central HPWH installations and file the report in this proceeding. 7. The Commission should direct SoCalGas, PG&E;, and SDG&E; to reserve $40 million in 2023 Cap-and-Trade allowance proceeds to augment funding for the SGIP HPWH subprogram consistent with each IOU’s respective percentage of their combined CARB allocation of Cap-and-Trade allowances. 8. 17 CCR Section 95893(d)(3) requires that gas Cap-and-Trade allowance proceeds must be used 1) for the primary benefit of retail natural gas ratepayers of each natural gas supplier, consistent with the goals of AB 32, and 2) to reduce GHG emissions. 9. Consistent with CCR Section 95893(d)(3), Cap-and-Trade allowance proceeds set aside in this decision should only be used to incentivize customers in the service territory of the funding gas utility. 10. Consistent with CCR Section 95893(d)(3), Cap-and-Trade allowance proceeds should not be used to fund HPWH incentives for existing all-electric customers who do not have natural gas service. 11. The Commission should direct SoCalGas, PG&E;, and SDG&E; to each file a Tier 1 Advice Letter within 30 days of the date of the issuance of this decision establishing a new balancing account to track all Cap-and-Trade allowance proceeds set aside pursuant to this decision, as well as any interest accrued on those proceeds. 12. The Commission should direct SoCalGas, PG&E;, and SDG&E; to transfer Cap-and-Trade allowance proceed funds to SCE as described in this decision. 13. The Commission should direct the statewide SGIP HPWH PA/PI, in cooperation with SCE to allocate each gas corporation’s contributed Cap-and
* * *
R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mph126 Trade allowance proceeds funds to HPWH customer classes using the percentages set forth in Table 6 of this decision. 14. The Commission should direct SCE to return to any Cap-and-Trade allowance proceeds that have not been allocated as of January 1, 2026 to the respective gas corporations contributing these funds (PG&E;, SoCalGas, and SDG&E;), and should direct these gas corporations to return those unspent funds to their respective ratepayers as part of the 2026 or 2027 Climate Credit. 15. The Commission should delegate authority to Energy Division staff to reduce a gas utility’s SGIP HPWH allocation from 2023 Cap-and-Trade allowance proceeds if necessary to ensure that the average residential customer’s Climate Credit is at least equal to residential customers’ annual Cap-and-Trade program costs. 16. The Commission should direct the SGIP HPWH PA/PI to undertake all necessary actions to develop and implement the SGIP HPWH program in accordance with this decision including to: a. Execute non-disclosure agreements regarding customer data and program issues with the implementer entities for the TECH Initiative and the BUILD program no later than 90 days after it has executed its contract with SCE; b. Submit a Tier 2 advice letter containing a SGIP HPWH Handbook reflecting the requirements adopted here, and reflecting additional Commission Staff guidance as necessary, no later than 120 days after executing its contract with SCE; c. Offer a digitized tool to contractors and retailers to capture end user installation data, confirm incentive eligibility and provide timely payment; d. Determine an appropriate SGIP HPWH incentive layering approach, including potentially a contractor pass-through incentive, in consultation with Commission Staff;
* * *
R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mph127 e. Adhere to the guiding principles adopted in D.21-11-002 when developing the incentive layering approach; f. Adhere to the additional guidance on incentive layering adopted in this decision; g. Develop a standardized protocol to estimate residential central and commercial unitary HPWH system incentives, which shall include: (1) methods to calculate project’s capacity; (2) a methodology for establishing a project’s non-load shifting baseline; (3) a standard set of normalization factors (i.e., outdoor temperature, etc.); and (4), a methodology for calculating greenhouse (GHG) emission reductions, refining the incentive and load shifting approaches proposed by Staff when doing so, as needed; h. Develop and implement a protocol to ensure that each gas utility’s portion of the $40 million in gas Cap-and-Trade allowance proceeds authorized in this decision for the SGIP HPWH program is only used to incentivize natural gas customers in the service territory of the funding gas utility, including developing a method to ensure that these monies are not used to fund HPWH incentives for existing all-electric customers who do not have natural gas service; i. Propose a standard hour-by-hour avoided GHG emissions value that residential central HPWH incentive applicants must incorporate into their system designs; j. Provide participating contractors installing residential unitary HPWH systems using SGIP incentives with information regarding the rationale for the TOU requirement, ensure that the contractors inform the customer of this requirement, and notify the relevant IOU about the HPWH installation; k. Ensure that customers installing residential unitary HPWH systems have been enrolled on a TOU rate prior to issuing the incentive payment;
* * *
R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mph128 l. Return any unallocated funds as of December 31, 2025 to SCE within 30 days for repayment to ratepayers pursuant to Section 379.6(a)(2); m. Collaborate with other HPWH incentive programs to support training contractors on the beneficial impact of TOU responsiveness. n. In collaboration with Commission Staff, offer an optional training and/or networking opportunity for contractors on demand response opportunities involving HPWHs as these become available, as discussed in section 14; o. Include in customer application forms a question allowing customers to express interest in future demand response opportunities that target HPWHs and that may provide additional bill savings; p. Describe in its implementation plan its proposed method to implement the demand response requirement adopted in this decision, as discussed in section 14; and, q. Provide contractors with information regarding qualified demand response programs including third-party aggregator offerings as discussed in section 14. 17. The Commission should authorize the SGIP HPWH PA to: a. Submit a Tier 1 advice letter to reallocate the reserved equity unitary and residential general market unitary funds, if not fully subscribed within three years after issuance of this decision, and any other unspent funds within our adopted budget to any customer class that is oversubscribed, including towards any qualifying technology introduced in the market that was not available at the start of the program; b. Submit a Tier 2 advice letter to propose additional residential unitary and central HPWH system product qualification pathways to those adopted here, using, for any additional residential central pathways, the CBEC-RES and ENERGY STAR® 2.0 requirements as guidelines;
* * *
R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mph129 c. Submit a Tier 2 advice letter once the AHRI Standard 1430 is adopted to propose this as a residential unitary HPWH system communication port pathway, if the PA believes this is appropriate; d. Submit a Tier 1 advice letter proposing modifications to the residential unitary HPWH system kicker incentive, as appropriate; and, e. Submit a Tier 2 advice letter at any time proposing reductions to the incentive levels adopted in this decision. 18. The Commission should clarify that distribution and service line upgrade costs required to serve installed HPWH systems are ineligible for SGIP HPWH incentives. 19. The Commission should direct the IOUs to: a. Address costs related to distribution and service line upgrades required to serve installed HPWH systems as Electric Service Line Allowance costs under Tariff Rule 15 and Rule 16; b. Classify distribution or service line upgrade costs to support SGIP HPWH installations in residential buildings in excess of those provided for under the Rule 15 and Rule
* * *
## _16 Electric Service Line Allowance as common facility_
costs, referring to this as Common Treatment for Excess
* * *
HPWH Costs; c. Recover Common Treatment for Excess HPWH Costs in the same manner as directed in D.11-07-029 for Common Treatment for Excess Plug-in Electric Vehicles Charging Costs, sunsetting this treatment with the termination of the SGIP HPWH program; d. Each provide to the SGIP HPWH PA/PI information regarding the method for contractors installing HPWH systems using residential unitary incentives to communicate to the utility that such a system has been installed;
* * *
R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mph130 e. Each ensure that customers installing HPWH systems using residential unitary incentives are enrolled in a TOU rate no later than 30 days after the utility has been notified by the contractor of the installation; and, f. Undertake the fund transfers and other steps outlined in section 9.3 to establish the SGIP HPWH PA/PI and to ensure the swift sharing of customer data needed to ensure program success. 20. The Commission should make commercial central HPWH systems ineligible for SGIP incentives. 21. The Commission should waive the existing equity budget eligibility requirement that single-family homes be subject to resale restrictions or presumed resale restrictions for purposes of SGIP HPWH incentives. 22. The Commission should adopt the four non-binding guiding principles on HPWH incentive layering adopted in D.21-11-002 for application to SGIP HPWH incentives. 23. The Commission should direct the SGIP HPWH PA/PI to take the following actions regarding training and workforce development: a. Educate contractors about the SGIP load shifting requirements; b. Develop and implement an eligible contractor list as described in section 11.3; c. Require contractors to provide the following before issuing an incentive payment: i. Proof that the installers of the HPWH system(s) have completed a relevant state-certified apprenticeship program or appropriate HPWH installation trainings as discussed in section 11.3; ii. Proof of permit application with the Authority Having Jurisdiction for HPWH installations;
* * *
R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mph131 d. Work with the SGIP evaluator to assess what workforce information is already being collected regarding SGIP contractors and determine, in consultation with Commission Staff, what might be appropriate additional workforce and training information to collect; e. Undertake, as determined appropriate in consultation with the SGIP HPWH evaluator and Commission Staff, a “low touch” method to collect additional appropriate SGIP workforce information; and, f. Cooperate with any larger HPWH workforce data tracking efforts undertaken using GHG emission allowance auction revenues, any TECH Initiative and/or BUILD program evaluation, and/or any similar umbrella evaluation effort. 24. The Commission should adopt the following guidance for evaluation of the SGIP HPWH program: a. The SGIP evaluator should summarize all benefits achieved by a SGIP funded HPWH. These benefits should include, but are not limited to, the total GHG reductions achieved by load shifting, including reductions in therms or kilowatt hours, and the peak reduction benefits compared to a non-load shifting HPWH; b. When layering of SGIP HPWH incentives and other incentives towards a single HPWH system occurs, the non load shifting benefits (i.e., the efficiency benefits) of SGIP funded HPWHs should be attributed to those other programs; similarly, the load shifting benefits achieved by SGIP funded HPWHs should not be attributed to other incentive programs; c. The SGIP HPWH PA should collect the information listed in section 11.2, as well as the type of refrigerant used in each project, to assist with evaluation of the program, modified as deemed necessary in consultation with Commission staff; d. The HPWH evaluation funds authorized in this decision should supplement the larger SGIP evaluation budgets
* * *
R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mph132 adopted in D.20-01-021. Evaluation activities for SGIP HPWH should be grouped with the other SGIP evaluation activities under the same contract. Commission Staff should take all necessary steps to ensure this. Within the evaluation budgets adopted here and in D.20-01-021, Commission Staff should be authorized to modify the SGIP 2021 – 2025 evaluation plan at any time to advance the goals of a complete and efficient evaluation process, including modifying the timing and scope of individual SGIP evaluation reports. If, given this direction, there are additional means to coordinate evaluation of the SGIP HPWH program with evaluation of the benefits of the TECH Initiative, the BUILD program and/or other relevant HPWH programs, Commission Staff should explore and, if appropriate, undertake such coordination if this serves to avoid duplication of evaluation efforts, and/or leverage personnel, database, and other resources; and, e. The SGIP evaluator shall undertake the other actions regarding evaluation summarized in section 12.3. 25. The Commission should authorize a SGIP HPWH program post installation inspection protocol that requires permits for each HPWH installation, that contractors upload photo documentation of the installation, and, if there are serious or repeated issues with an individual contractor, that ensures that the SGIP HPWH PA/PI conducts additional on-site inspections and follow-up to ensure quality installation. 26. The Commission should deny SoCalGas’s Motion to Strike. 27. The Commission should require all customers receiving SGIP HPWH incentives to enroll in a qualified demand response program, defined as a CAISO market-integrated supply-side demand response program that counts for resource adequacy. Customers should remain enrolled in the qualified demand response program for a minimum of three years.
* * *
R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mph133 28. The Commission should consider the need for additional demand response pilots or programs involving or targeting grid-supporting HPWH, including HPWH with installed CTA-2045 ports and communication modules, in the upcoming demand response application proceeding. O R D E R IT IS ORDERED that: 1. The Self-Generation Incentive Program heat pump water heater budgets, incentives and program requirements contained in Appendix A are adopted. 2. The Staff Proposal for a single statewide Self-Generation Incentive Program heat pump water heater program administrator/program implementer described in section 9.1 is adopted. 3. The Self-Generation Incentive Program (SGIP) heat pump water heater (HPWH) program administrative budget is capped at 10 percent of the SGIP HPWH budget, or $4,467,000 million. If the selected bid to administer and implement the SGIP HPWH program is below the 10 percent cap, after deducting the budget authorized for SGIP HPWH evaluation in this decision, the difference shall be reallocated equally across incentive budgets. 4. The percent of the Self-Generation Incentive Program (SGIP) heat pump water heater (HPWH) residential general market and residential equity budgets that may fund residential central HPWH incentives is capped at 40 percent of each of these budgets. 5. R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mph144 15. Distribution and service line upgrade costs required to serve installed heat pump water heater (HPWH) systems are ineligible for Self-Generation Incentive Program (SGIP) incentives. Commercial central HPWH systems are ineligible for SGIP incentives. 16. Distribution and service line upgrade costs required to serve installed heat pump water heater systems are eligible for and shall be addressed as Electric Service Line Allowance costs under Tariff Rule 15 and Rule 16. 17. The four non-binding guiding principles on heat pump water heater (HPWH) incentive layering adopted in Decision 21-11-002 are adopted for application to Self-Generation Incentive Program HPWH incentives. 18. The following guidance for evaluation of the Self-Generation Incentive Program (SGIP) heat pump water heater (HPWH) program is adopted: a. The SGIP HPWH program evaluator shall summarize all the benefits achieved by a SGIP funded HPWH. These benefits should include, but are not limited to, the total greenhouse gas reductions achieved by the SGIP funded load shifting HPWH, including reductions in therms or kilowatt hours, and the peak reduction benefits compared to a non-load shifting HPWH; b. When layering of SGIP HPWH incentives and other incentives towards a single HPWH system occurs, the non load shifting benefits (i.e., the efficiency benefits) of SGIP funded HPWHs should be attributed to those other programs; similarly, the load shifting benefits achieved by SGIP funded HPWHs should not be attributed to other incentive programs; c. The SGIP HPWH program administrator / program implementer (PA/PI) shall collect the information listed in section 11.2, and the type of refrigerant used in each HPWH project, to assist with evaluation of the program, augmented or modified as deemed necessary;
* * *
R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mph145 d. The HPWH evaluation funds authorized in this decision shall supplement the larger SGIP evaluation budgets adopted in Decision 20-01-021. Evaluation activities for SGIP HPWH shall be grouped with the other SGIP evaluation activities under the same contract. Commission Staff shall take all necessary steps to ensure this. Within the evaluation budgets adopted here and in D.20-01-021, Commission Staff are authorized to modify the SGIP 2021 – 2025 evaluation plan at any time to advance the goals of a complete and efficient evaluation process, including modifying the timing and scope of individual SGIP evaluation reports. If, given this direction, there are additional means to coordinate evaluation of the SGIP HPWH program with evaluation of the benefits of the TECH Initiative, the BUILD program and/or other relevant HPWH programs, Commission Staff should explore and, if appropriate, undertake such coordination if this serves to avoid duplication of evaluation efforts, and/or leverage personnel, database, and other resources; and, e. The SGIP evaluator shall undertake the other actions regarding HPWH evaluation summarized in section 12.3. 19. Existing Self-Generation Incentive Program (SGIP) Handbook rules not otherwise modified in this decision apply to the SGIP heat pump water heater program. 20. APPENDIX A

SELF-GENERATION INCENTIVE PROGRAM HEAT PUMP WATER HEATER ADOPTED BUDGETS AND PROGRAM REQUIREMENTS
* * *
R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mphA2 Self-Generation Incentive Program Heat Pump Water Heater Budget and Program Requirements 1. Budget Allocations The adopted sub-budgets for the $44,670,000 SGIP HPWH budget adopted in D.19 09-027 and D.20-01-021 are shown in the tables below. In addition, twenty percent of each of the general market residential and equity residential budgets ($3.8 million for each budget) is reserved for unitary system incentives. Incentives for residential central HPWH systems are capped at 40 percent of each residential budget until such time as a preliminary review of these technologies’ load shifting performance is completed. Electrical panel incentives are capped at 30 percent of both the general market and equity residential budgets ($5,728,927 each). SGIP HPWH Budget Allocation by Activity Activity Amount Program Administration: 4,467,000 HPWH Incentives: $40,203,000 Total SGIP HPWH Budget: $44,670,000
* * *
SGIP HPWH Incentive Budget Allocation by Customer Class Customer Class Percent Amount General Market Residential Unitary & Central HPWHs 47.4 $19,040,588 Equity Residential Unitary & Central HPWHs 47.4 $19,040,587 Commercial Unitary HPWHs (including small business unitary HPWH systems) 5.3 $2,121,825 Total SGIP HPWH Incentive Budget 100.0 $40,203,000
* * *
Existing SGIP rules not modified here continue to apply to the SGIP HPWH program. Any retail electric or gas distribution class of customer (industrial, agricultural,
* * *
R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mphA3 commercial, or residential) of PG&E;, SCE, SoCalGas, or SDG&E; is eligible to be a Host Customer that receives incentives through SGIP. 2. Residential Unitary HPWHs Residential unitary HPWHs are defined as both integrated and split HPWHs with a total nominal compressor output power of six kilowatt or less installed to serve a single household in a single-family, duplex, or multi-family property. Eligible residential unitary HPWHs must meet the following requirements:75 2.1 Appliance Requirements  Residential unitary HPWHs must be identified as a CEC JA-13 compliant water heater by the CEC (which ensures that they are equipped with a thermostatic mixing valve).  Residential unitary HPWHs must be identified by NEEA’s most recent qualified product list as having a CTA-2045 Compliant Communication Port or must be certified as a Connected Water Heater product under the Version 4.0 ENERGY STAR® Product Specification for Residential Water Heaters.76 2.2 Installation Requirements  Residential unitary HPWHs must be installed in compliance with the CEC’s JA-13 installation specifications and must be correctly sized according to JA-13 first hour recovery requirements.  Installers are required to report all HPWH installations using SGIP incentives to the appropriate utility, which is required to enroll the participant on a TOU rate within 30 days.77
* * *
## If they meet all technology, installation and load shifting requirements, combination hot water
heating/space heating systems, and 120-volt HPWH systems are eligible for the residential unitary HPWH
* * *
incentives.
* * *
## _76 Available as of November 18, 2021 at:_
https://www.energystar.gov/sites/default/files/ENERGY%20STAR%20Version%204.0%20Water%20Hea
* * *
ters%20Final%20Specification%20and%20Partner%20Commitments_0.pdf.
* * *
## _77 Customers installing a residential unitary HPWH using SGIP incentives may enroll in any TOU rate, not_
just an SGIP-approved rate (see D.19-09-001).
* * *
R.20-05-012 COM/CR6/mphA4 2.3 Load Shifting Requirements  Residential unitary HPWHs must be programmed to execute the basic load-up and light shed demand management functionality as defined in JA-13. This demand management functionality will signal the HPWH to store thermal energy during certain times to avoid electricity usage at different times  Residential unitary HPWHs must execute the basic load-up and light shed demand management response based on the local utility’s TOU rates.  Customers that install SGIP funded residential unitary HPWHs shall enroll in a qualified demand response program, defined as a CAISO market-integrated supply side demand response program that counts for resource adequacy. 2.4 Incentive Structure & Value  Residential unitary HPWH incentives are based on the energy storage capacity of a 50-gallon tank volume and a temperature setpoint of 135°F.  Residential unitary HPWH incentive are calculated using an estimated 3.1-kilowatt hour energy storage capacity regardless of tank size to simplify program administration.  The initial SGIP HPWH incentive value for general market residential customers is set at $1,000 per kilowatt hour.  The initial SGIP HPWH incentive value for equity residential customers is set at $1,350 per kilowatt hour.  A $1,500 low GWP kicker incentive for HPWHs that utilize a refrigerant with a GWP of 150 or less is provided.  An additional $700 incentive is provided for HPWH with storage capacities of 55 gallons or more.  Customers may receive SGIP incentives regardless of the type of HPWH system previously installed, if all other SGIP requirements are met.
* * *
### Electrical Panel Upgrade and Electrical Service Incentives
 General market residential customers may access an electrical panel upgrade incentive
* * *
that covers 50 percent of general market customer costs, capped at $2,000.  Equity residential customers may access an electric panel upgrade incentive that covers 100 percent of electric panel upgrade costs, capped at $4,000.
* * *

* * *
Maximum Unitary HPWH Incentives for a HPWH by Customer Class78 Customer Class Unitary HPWH Incentive Low-GWP Kicker Incentive

## _55 Gallon_
Capacity
